                            <div class="player-info-box-header "><div class="player-info-box-name"><h1>Mark Reynolds</h1><div class="player-info-box-name-team">&nbsp;</div></div><div class="player-info-box-item">Age: 34</div><div class="player-info-box-item">Bats/Throws: R/R</div>
        <div class="player-info-box-item">6' 2" / 220</div>
        <div class="player-info-box-pos">1B/3B</div></div>
                        <div class="player-info-bio">

            <div class="catchall300" style='width:300px; overflow:hidden;float:right;'><!-- RoS_300x250 --> <div id ='div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13' style='width:300px; height:250px; overflow:hidden;'><script type='text/javascript'>if ($('#div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13').is(':visible')) {googletag.cmd.push(function () { googletag.display('div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13'); });}</script></div></div>

    <tr>
        
        <tr id="so2" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Year in Review: </b>Everybody knew Reynolds had some crazy raw power, but the 44 homers were definitely unexpected. The third baseman also added 24 steals in 33 attempts to become a rare 40-20 threat. Overall, Reynolds hit .260/.349/.543 and posted a .284 ISO in 578 at-bats. The 223 strikeouts (an MLB record) are certainly ugly, but most leagues don't penalize fantasy players for them. Extremely durable, Reynolds has appeared in 152+ games in each of the past two seasons. His 3.9 WAR suggests that he is an extremely valuable "real-life" player, as well as one of the top third-base options in fantasy baseball.<br /><br /><b>The Year Ahead: </b>Reynolds is one of the few legitimate third basemen that can provide speed, as well as power, which makes him extremely valuable. He may not slug 40+ homers again, but 30-35 dingers would be a reasonable expectation. A total of 100 or more RBI could also be met, especially if the lineup around him improves for 2010. Underrated in many leagues at the beginning of '09, Reynolds will not fall under many radars in '10. With 28 appearances at first base in '09, he has added versatility over other top third basemen like David Wright and Evan Longoria. Thankfully, fantasy baseball doesn't care that he's a below-average fielder at both positions, according to UZR. (Marc Hulet)</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so3" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>A 42.3% strikeout rate is impressive for many reasons. It's impressive that a team left him out there for almost 600 plate appearances with that high of a strikeout rate, for one. In a related note, it's impressive that he managed a wRC+ of 100 in a season that saw him hit under .200 and strike out in two-fifths of his at-bats. His patience was still there (13.9 BB% in 2010, 11.4% career), and the power still sparkled (.234 ISO in 2010, .241 career). A year with fewer strikeouts and better luck on the batted ball (.257 BABIP) might mean a better batting average, but that batting average will smell more like Adam Dunn than Ichiro Suzuki. In OBP leagues or H2H leagues where you can punt batting average, he should be a stud, but in most standard leagues, the "Adam Dunn of third base" is looking more and more like his ceiling.  (Eno Sarris)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>The King of the K would have broken the strikeout record with a full slate of at-bats, but it's a testament to his power and patience that he still managed to put up average production while striking out in two-fifths of his at-bats.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so4" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Despite changing leagues, Reynolds' three-true outcome warpath rages on. This time, Reynolds walked, fanned, or homered in just under half of his plate appearances. In fact, Reynolds' career mark stands at 50.1 percent. In spite of all the homers (37), Reynolds was as abysmal as ever at the hot corner, registering an appalling 28.2 runs BELOW replacement level, more than doubling his career output to date. Much like Adam Dunn, teams can put up with Reynolds when the fielding effort is there, but when it's not, players like this are hard pressed to break even value-wise, no matter how many runs they produce, and Reynolds' 0.3 WAR in 2011 is evidence. A move to first base may be forthcoming. (Brandon Warne)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>If you've spent your entire draft chasing steals and saves, Reynolds is your guy. A virtual lock for 30 home runs, Reynolds can be a good late-round guy if your batting average is strong. Advanced-stat leagues will appreciate Reynolds a bit more too, but be wary of a move off third base. </td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so5" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Mike Podhorzer <a href="http://www.fangraphs.com/fantasy/index.php/mark-reynolds-enters-the-land-of-cleve/">noted</a> that the change to Progressive Field could negatively impact Reynolds’ 2013 home run production and increase the number of times he strikes out. Additionally, the guy hit .221 in each of the last two seasons, making the now slower-footed corner infielder a liability in at least two of the five standard roto categories. With more strikeouts and fewer home runs in his new digs, he's even less attractive. For the owners who just can’t stay away, Reynolds could be a fit for teams in search of some cheap power late in their drafts despite hitting 14 fewer long balls in 2011 than 2010. (Alan Harrison)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Reynolds signed a one-year deal with the Cleveland Indians this offseason, a move that could potentially deflate his fantasy value.  </td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so6" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Reynolds is losing his only elite skill. Last season, his slugging percentage dropped below .400 for the first time in his career and his isolated slugging percentage dropped under .200 for the first time as well. It's not like he played in tough parks either, as he accumulated most of his at-bats in Yankee Stadium. It's reached a point where Reynolds' flaws are starting to outweigh his strength. Even if he finds himself in a position for playing time -- and he should be in the running for some of the work at first for the Brewers -- it's tough to employ him in fantasy unless you really need power in an on-base percentage league. Even then, his home run numbers have fallen off recently, and the Brewers might be looking to the future if things don't work out in the early going. (<a href="https://twitter.com/Chris_Cwik">Chris Cwik</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Reynolds power declined last season despite receiving most of his playing time in a strong offensive park. The flaws are beginning to outweigh the positives.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so7" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>In his power prime from 2009-2011, Mark Reynolds hit 113 home runs, an average of 38 per season. That power has eroded in recent seasons. Despite nearly 500 plate appearances per season over the last three, Reynolds has averaged just 22 home runs per season in that time. Meanwhile, 2014 may have been the last season Reynolds earned a full-time job. This offseason, he joined the Cardinals, who are set with Matt Carpenter and Matt Adams in the corner spots. Both Carpenter and Adams are left-handed hitters, which makes the right-handed Reynolds a likely starter against difficult lefties. However, Reynolds has not shown dramatic splits in his career, so there is no reason to expect his performance per plate appearance will improve dramatically in a reduced role. And now that his power totals have declined, his likely .220 or worse batting average becomes that much harder to swallow. (Scott Spratt)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>In his prime, Reynolds routinely hit 30 or more home runs per season. His recent power decline, a terrible batting average, and a likely reserve role with the Cardinals in 2015 renders him undraftable in fantasy.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Once upon a time, Reynolds hit 44 home runs and stole 24 bases. That was 2009. More recently, the results have been... not good. The journeyman is preparing to join his sixth team in five years -- the Colorado Rockies. It's the best case scenario for a power hitter with serious contact problems. Pitches have less movement at a high altitude which should help Reynolds' strikeout rate. If he's going to experience a renaissance season, it will be at Coors Field. He'll probably form a platoon with Ben Paulsen, but it's worth noting that Reynolds has small platoon splits. Paulsen has a short major league track record, so there's a possible scenario where Reynolds becomes the everyday guy. Just don't hold your breath. (Brad Johnson)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Were you ready to give up on this guy? I was. Now that he's a Rockie, we'll need to pay attention one more time. He's exactly the type of hitter who could thrive in the Coors Field power oasis.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>When he was in Arizona, Mark Reynolds was a very useful player. In his four years there, from 2007-2010, he was worth 8.1 WAR. In the following six years, he has played for six teams, and been worth 1.9 WAR, which is quite interesting considering that in 2014 he was worth 1.6 WAR. Said differently, in five of the past six years, Reynolds has been a replacement-level player. Still, he manages to find work. He doesn't have much of a platoon split - for his career, he has been an above-average hitter against both lefties (109 wRC+) and righties (103 wRC+). He has enough pop to be a threat, and while he strikes out plenty, he doesn't have a fatal flaw. If you look at his career defensive summary, you'd think he was a terrible fielder, given his -63.4 Fld. But much of that stems from being allowed to play third base for longer than he was capable. He's been just fine as a first baseman since shifting there full-time - from 2012-2016, he has posted a -1.4 UZR and a +2 DRS. In the past four seasons, he's even managed to log 15 harmless innings at second base. Mark Reynolds is the spitting image of the phrase, "he's fine." Regardless of the minor league deal he signed with the Rockies, nothing he did in 2016 showed a marked degradation of his skills. He'll eventually sign somewhere, and while he's not a player you need to draft, he'll probably be worth having on your bench at some point during the season.<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Mark Reynolds will happily provide you with league-average offensive production and not much else. Never truly a starter or a bench player, Reynolds always seems to turn up somewhere, perhaps even on your bench.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
    </tr>

            <td align="left" style="width: 175px">
